Of treatment of the anus not perforated:
Infants born and their anuses are not perforated, which are closed with a slender and opilated instrument. Then let the midwife pierce it with her finger; and if not, pierce that with a pointed spatula, and be careful not to touch the clitoris.
Then place wool soaked in wine and oil over it; and then heal that with ointment so that it is healed.
But if you are afraid it's opilated, then place a straw through the opening for a few days, and wait until the child wants to urinate.
And then let the opilation of the anus happen because of hardening of a fistula or of an abscess; therefore let that hardening be cut into and then be healed with what we have said of the treatment and of the plaster.
Of treatment of fistulas that go down from the anus:
The fistulas which go down in the part of the anus with pus are narrow and thick, which go down close to the anus or into the space of one part.
And it is a fistula or many.
And when that node is old and corroded by the white or yellow pus subtilely, and sometimes some of these fistulas penetrate.
And when they penetrate, they are known by what is coming out of them by their egress and by windiness; and sometimes the patient gets sick from the egress, and a lot comes out of them with wind.
And sometimes those fistulas penetrate into the space into the bladder, and into the meat of the vagina; and sometimes they penetrate the joint of the vagina and into the notch of the womb.
And when the fistula penetrates into the anus and doesn' t penetrate, it is that you put your finger in the anus, and insert a slender instrument of horn or iron in the fistula when it isn't hard.
But if in that there is hardness, then insert a slender instrument of lead or of horse-hair into that so that you feel the instrument or the hair in your finger.
And if you don't feel that at all nor process any cause of exudation or windiness or pus, just as we have said, then know that it is not penetrating, and then cut to the treatment.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the meat of the vagina, then its sign is exudation of urine from that and prohibition of it to go out because the place is closed by medicine.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the vagina or into the bone and feels pain in the circumference, then its sign is that the instrument comes into contact with the bone here if in the joint it isn' t hard; and pain in the circumference of the part; and that from there comes putrefaction sometimes; and that does not work in that medicine, nor is it healed with ointment.
And in all those penetration of ulcers there is no cure for everything, and the cure for them is labor and vanity of foolish doctors about whom they are confident.
But in that which does not penetrate and is not ancient, healing with iron is expected according to what I will tell you, and what I have already tried and tested.
And it is that the patient lies on his back and lifts his legs between them, his thighs are bent towards his stomach, and then the surgeon tries to insert a probe made of lead or copper if there is a tortuous ulcer, into the location where he suspects the fistula is.
And if the patient has pain around his neck, then you must insert your little finger into his neck.
And if you feel with your finger that the probe has already penetrated by itself, then know for certain that it is penetrating; and then do not labor on it, for there is no cure for it, as we have said.
But some say that it is cured in some people who are constipated.
And the medicine by which healing is expected in him is that you apply a subtle cautery according to the size of the fistula, as it is mentioned above, and insert the lit cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the neck; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all the flesh attached to the fistula that resembles a feather cane have been burnt.
And then treat that with poultices wrapped in cloth until all the flesh that is attached has healed, and then cure that with medicines which generate and solidify flesh, and if it is healed, well; but if not, then it will not be healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters but does not penetrate your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a thin layer of flesh or membrane, then cut the fistula where it touches the surface of the skin, and dry up the skin from the beginning of the fistula; and you see that it dries by means of the probe until the incision reaches the place where the end of the probe is, and through the probe it is freed and drained.
And then clean out those flesh-like fistulas which are like a quill of a feather, and do not let them remain for any cause.
But if blood flows out between you and your operation when you touch those flesh, then the milker, which is a very useful instrument, must cauterize that with fire or with a sharp medicine; for cautery with fire produces two good results: it is an abscission of those attached flesh, and it dries up the blood by exsiccation.
And then cure that with poultices wrapped in cloth or with soft linen until the place becomes hard, and then cure the wound with ointments which generate and solidify hard flesh.
And these medicines are for constriction and exsiccation; when it is restored, then it is healed and you need not fear a recurrence.
But if the fistula already penetrates inside the rectum and is elongated from there to the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and probe with it.
And so if the probe is touched and between you and it there is a membrane or flesh which is close to penetration, then it is not an operation on him but according to the way of confidence and hope.
And this cause is treated in him by one of three ways: either cauterize that as we have said; or dry up that place until you can reach the cavity so that it may be possible for you to insert the tools and apply the treatment; nor should you cut into the skin containing the neck, and make the patient suffer against his will and without his consent; and then cure that with what we have said, for the vein there will heal quickly. Or lengthen that membrane and clean out that pus which you dry up near the rectum, either with a probe or another instrument with a sharp end, and then clean out those flesh-like fistulas which are in the incision by means of scraping; and strive to make the restoration of the whole wound by cutting into it, and if that outlet is not solidified above, and the fistula remains open at the exit near the rectum, then pus will flow more quickly through the patient. And sometimes the fistula is finished in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is on the side of the rectum around the surface of the body with the skin and at the edge of the rectum, then take a probe of a perforated end, which is like a needle for piercing ears according to this form:
and insert it into that vein wrapped in a thread or around those; and then insert the probe with the needle into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
But if it penetrates at the edge of the rectum near the inside, then you extract that needle from there as you insert your finger into the rectum; and pull out the end of the needle and tighten both ends, and keep them tight for a day or two, and when you have pierced the flesh with the needle more deeply, loosen that again so that those flesh are pierced between both ends of the needle and they are pulled in.
And then cure the wound until it is solidified and healed.
But if the outlet of the fistula is not penetrated, then make that penetration by whatever way you are prepared for it, except in the rectum, for it is not for you to do what pierces the skin. And then heal that with which it is healed.
The form of a thorn with which the fistula is split: the curve of which is very acute and the other part is not acute, so that it does not pierce what is not necessary, as it touches.
Of suturing hemorrhoids in which blood flows at the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are according to two ways, for either they are between the rectum like that which is of veins, or those which are like fat with pus. And they are small and great, and the blood flows from them at any time; they are swollen, and outside the rectum, and at their ends.
But those which are outside the rectum are according to the months of little moisture, of which flows citrine water or a little blood with continuous body, and they are according to the colour of the body.
And the cure for that which is inside the rectum is to command the patient to sit and strain so that the rectum dries up, and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you hold it and pull with your nails, and then pierce those deep into their roots.
But if the nails are not in them because of their moisture and softness, then take those with an asp or a ramshorn or with a flap of wool, and pull them out with your fingers, and then pierce them and powder over them some powdered caustics after you have probed them, so that they are as cauterized; or cauterize them according to what is mentioned in the chapter on cauterizing. And then cure those with the other cure until they are healed.
And if the rectum does not obey the ejection, then christen the patient with a small Christ's nail, and let him be washed with that which is in the rectum and that he strain it out gently when the patient defecates.
But if the hemorrhoids protrude from the anus, then the cause of them is laziness, and you should take those with your finger, or take those with ointment, and insert those, and then cure those with what we have said until they are healed.
And whoever wants to treat them, he should administer a suture in the following way: take some wool wrapped in a needle and stick it through the hemorrhoids at their upper parts, and insert the needle into the root of the other part, and wrap both ends of the wool around the part closest to the needle, and then pull the hemorrhoid with strong pressure, and squeeze the wool and take out the needle.
And do this to all of them, and leave one of them uncovered and un-sewed, so that the excess blood can flow out through it.
And then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or ceratum over the anus, and tell him to rest. And let that remain until it is healed, then cure them with ointment or another medicine until they are healed.
And when they are old and medicine does not help them, then you should open them with a spatulum or your finger until they become moist and the pus is drained from above, and you should prevent them from becoming hard; and then cure them until they are healed.
Then you should open them more violently than the first time so that there is more bleeding and they become raw, and then cure them until they are healed.
Know that a hernia is crucial, and it is not unless it is a round knot according to the color of the body resembling the head of a nail, and it occurs all over the body and mostly in the lower parts of the legs and the groin, and due to this you should feel pain.
And so you should insert what is in the loop of the nail, and hold it with forceps or ointment, and remove it from its root; and then cure it.
And if you want, cauterize the second one that is mentioned in the previous chapter.
But a verruca is a small, asperous, round and fistulated protuberance on the surface of the body, and many times it occurs in the hands of children.
And its cure is that you stretch and insert it, or pierce it with a needle until it drains. And if you want, cauterize it, as we have said, with fire or acute medicine.
But a furuncle is a small, fluffy or fistulated protuberance on the surface of the body which occurs frequently in the armpits and the groin, and when it is rubbed it feels like an ant bite, and it occurs all around the body.
And its cure is that you take a feather of an eagle or a vulture, and make it strong, and place the feather over the furuncle so that the circle of the feather contains the furuncle; and then rotate your hand with the feather so that you insert what is in the loop of the furuncle, and place it in the flesh of the skin, and eradicate the furuncle from its root.
And if you want, do it with an iron needle or a needle made of steel according to this form:
Let the end of the needle be drawn out very thinly, blunt (so it is not pierced), wrapped so that above the finger it appears smooth and its revolution is complete.
And if you want, cauterize the furuncle as it is mentioned in the chapter on cautery.
On the form of instruments which are administered to give cautery:
Sometimes cautery is done with silver or brass or melted iron or beaten; and sometimes it is done with small or large instruments according to the treatment they are administered for, because that which is administered in the cure of small children should be small; and for those whose anuses are painful or swollen, their cauteries should be small.
The form of a great and few cauteries:
Let the end of the needle where the bag is tied be wide according to this manner; and let it have distinction where the bag is tied in that way as well.
And the end of it which is inserted into the anus be blunt  (so it is not open), drawn out very thinly; and in one side of it there are two holes, and in the other side a hole, and let the width of the hole be according to the thickness of the rays and plus thick a small one.
And let the bag in which the medicines are placed be made of animal bladder or sheep skin, and let its root be one palm long.
And let it be perforated by many holes so that when it is revolved the space between the holes is the thickness of the finger.
And then insert into those holes strong oil of oil which is made from ten oils, or surround it with that; and adjust with that the bag; and when it is placed in him the bag, let it be squeezed in the end of the cautery between two distinctions with a strong grip, and then cauterize with that each hole: However, the quality of cauterizing with that is that you fill the bag with medicine which has been warmed according to the quantity of the patient's tolerance; and beware that it is not too hot nor too cool.
And then the patient should recline on his back, and raise his legs up and place under him a bed or a plank; and then anoint the end of the cautery with oil or egg white or mucilage of fenugreek or something similar; and then insert it into the anus easily, and then the minister should take the bag and squeeze it with two fingers with strength until everything that is in the cautery is evacuated; and then take out the cautery and let the patient raise his legs to the wall.
And if the medicine forces it out, he should hold it as long as possible; and if he falls asleep from it for a whole night, it is better.
However, if you cauterize him for disorders of a swollen belly and its discharge, then you should repeat the cautery until it is healed.
And if in the anus there are hemorrhoids or painful lumps, then let the cautery be very thin and strong, of cauteries children are cauterized.
On the treatment of warts:
You already said in the division of the cure of warts what suited the member, and I said thus the cure of them with hand and medicines according to their complement.
Therefore, I say that warts are diversified according to what they are made of and according to the place where they occur.
And the causes by which many warts are made are thus with stone, or piercing of a spear, or blow with an arrow, or with a staff, or a similar object to these.
However, the warts which are on the body parts are thus with a wart which falls over the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the buttocks, and similarly in these of the members.
I will now explain the treatment for some diseases, through which you can extrapolate this reasoning and rule to other illnesses.
I will begin with simple diseases of the head, since the discussion of composite diseases will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when a simple plague occurs in the head and it is not a fracture, then consider the following: If it is due to the impact of a stone or something similar, and the vein has been ruptured and there is a large wound which you fear might become infected with pus, then immediately cut and bleed the cephalic vein, and do not delay; extract the blood according to the amount indicated by its color if no other cause prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood coming out of the wound is scarce.
And let the phlebotomy be on the side opposite to the affected part; and guard against repletion, wine, and heavy foods.
But if some cause prohibits phlebotomy due to weakness, then decrease the food intake and do not come near meat or wine.
And place on the wound, if it happens that there is pus and a boil has formed, a poultice soaked in rose oil or wine in which styptic herbs have been mixed.
But if he is certain of the pus, then place on the wound, if it is recent and his blood has not changed that powder which I will shortly mention.
And if the air has already changed its color, then place a dressing over it until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or caused by a spear or similar object, and its lips are not closed together by stitches, then close them with stitches as I will instruct later on.
But if the wound is uncovered and has exposed bone, and there is no retention except for a small bandage, then insert into it and treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh by applying poultices in the place of the vein; and command the patient to abstain from foods which are heavy: capers and their likes.
And if you find putrefaction in the vein and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut out all that rottenness and throw it away, and then treat it.
But if in these plagues there is an artery or vein from which blood can flow, and it has not been cut off by medicines, then probe for the artery; and if you find it uncut, then cut it open with a spatulum or ligature it; and if necessity forces you to do what we have said, then cauterize it so that the blood is cut off.
And if these plagues are simple and small, then the cause of them is cold; therefore you should powder those with this powder, because it consolidates them before the air changes them.
And the manner of the powder is this: take olive oil, one part; dragon's blood, two parts; and unburned ash, three parts; mix all together and sift it, then fill the wound with it and compress it with a good bandage so that it adheres to the wound, and let it coagulate with the blood.
And then tighten it above with powder and lye, and do not loosen it while it is like that unless there is pus; for the medicine will not be absorbed as long as it is unhealed.
And sometimes you can do this alone when you do not have dragon's blood at hand.
And indeed this method of operation is suitable for great wounds when you judge that it should be applied and the wounds are tight and recent, and the air has not altered them.
And if the air has altered them, and especially in summer time, then it is not intended to apply this powder; but instead cure them with what we have said, so that you place on them some cooked poultices, or place on them plasters made of barley flour mixed with water and honey so that they draw out the rottenness; and then cure them with the other treatment until they are healed.
But if a fracture in bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then extract it with a probe.
And I will instruct on the cure for fractures of the head when they are infected.
Of wounds to the neck:
But if a wound occurs in the neck, then it is not different in treatment; and between the wounds of the head when they are simple.
But if the nerve of the neck is cut or an artery, then if it is a nerve, there is no harm in treating it with anything that causes violent contraction, like vinegar and its likes which do not hurt nerves and constrict that. And do not place on the wound any cause of great heat, because the substance of nerves is heat and their continuation is in the brain, which is the source of all the members.
And if it is being treated with cold medicines, like being washed with tepid water, then some oil soaked in rose or non-rose water, and thujia when it is washed with tepid water and the other herbs are applied according to this manner, and moist poultices.
But if the wound is large, then administer stitches, or tie its lips together, and then cure it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep or there is pus in its bottom part, then bore it in its most depressed spot.
But if the artery is cut and blood flows from it, then separate or dry it out, or ligature it, or cauterize it if necessity forces you to do so.
But if the ligaments of the neck or the whole are cut and the veins of the neck are saved, then you tighten the lips of the vein with stitches on the bone of the neck, and do not touch it; bind that down to its natural shape and compress it with decent tightness, and dress it with poultices in which there is contraction and excitement until it is healed.
But if the wound has caused depression in the bone, then pierce it and if there are pieces of bone in it, extract all that you can; and what you cannot, dress it so that the wound putrefies, and let its separation be eased.
And if the wound is recent, then powder it with the powder which I have instructed you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then cure it with that which its recollection will come to you later in this chapter.
If it is a wound from a spear or knife, and you see its depth, then consider that if the patient has fever; then know that this wound is deadly.
And if it is not deep and the wound is recent with its blood, then you place on it in the first hour a powder, and do not tighten it so that the blood is not retained in the depth of it and returns to the heart and that it weakens the patient; but apply an attractive poultice, and loosen it twice a day.
And if the poultice is not at hand, then you bore a hole in the wound and insert a cotton swab so that it sucks out what there is in its humidity; and let the patient sleep on the wound so that what is inside it runs out.
But if the plague has passed for three days or more and no spasms occur in the patient, nor trembling of the heart, nor stricture of the bowels, and you see other good signs in him, then know that the plague is over; and then heal it with leeches and with other treatment until it is healed.
But if the healing of him is prevented and the opening remains for a long time, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore heal it through its own channel.
And if the plague is from an incision of a vein or artery and is simple in appearance on the surface of the foot or leg, then heal it with what has been said about stitching, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if there has been an impression in the bone and you see that it incises pieces of bone, then twist the plague and cut to extract those pieces, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then close the opening so that the plague may be cured; for the extraction of them is painful.
And in other plagues that occur in other members, the healing method is the same as what we have said before.
Of plagues of the belly:
The disposition that occurs in the belly is either great or small or intermediate.
And a small disruption is made difficult in another way, and one finds that the reduction of them is more difficult if you do not close the penetration of them into their place immediately, for it opens and swells so that the insertion of it is made difficult.
And therefore the best disruption is intermediate, for in that one the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, just as it is difficult in the two species mentioned above.
And know that stitching of the belly is according to four methods, but two stitches are common in stitching of the other plagues of the body.
And two other stitches are specific to other plagues but nonetheless more proper in the stitching of the belly.
I will return to you each one with exposition and declaration.
Therefore I say that when the plague is small and there has been caused some injury of the intestine because of it, and it is made difficult to reduce it, then it is in one of two ways: either by smallness of the disruption as we have said, or when the intestine has received inflammation from the chill of the air.
And therefore when that is so, then you should warm it so that you submerge a sponge or damp cloth in only tepid water, or that you gather in squinant water, and cyperum, and spic, and embed with that the intestine until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes it does this in wine in which there is stricture, and it is better than water alone for resolution of the inflammation.
And therefore when you see the inflammation resolved, then you dampen the intestine with water in which is mixed aloe and mallows, for by that the insertion of it is made easier with little pain.
But if the insertion of it is prevented by this cure, then you split the disruption a little with an instrument by which there are incised hemorrhoids.
And the first cure is better when it is not cut and there is no need for a bandage to dry up and deflate.
Of the instrument, know its form:
One side of it is bent acute, and the other side not acute, and the extremity subtle, and let its subtlety not be like a spathe but rather obtuse a little.
And therefore when the plague is amplified and sees outside the intestine, then you should ensure that its reduction is according to its natural form in its proper place, if it is possible; for that is better.
But when the disruption is large and it is in the lower part of the belly, then you should have the patient lie on his back and place his legs above; and if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you should have him lie on his side with his head and higher leg up.
And likewise if the disruption is in one of the two parts of the belly, then pay your attention and your consideration always to that so that the part in which there is the plague be higher than the other.
And therefore you should administer that in great plagues and in intermediate plagues.
And in small plagues, pause what is prepared for you and let the minister be between your fingers, who has the disruption all under his hand and adjust the lips of it, and let him uncover that to which there occurs stitching from any cause whatever.
And this is the way of common stitching: take the needle, or count of needles according to the amount of the amplitude of the plague; and let there be left from the extremity of the disruption of the finger, and tighten the needle one, or else put thread in both extremities of the cord, with two extremities of the ciphac that is below the cord of the fingers, into which you make penetration of that party; and you have added two extremities of the cord and two extremities of the ciphac, and so they are made four turns.
And let there be tightening with double thread around the circuit of the needle, and sometimes from two parts together, and let you add both lips and the plague by decent aggregation; and let there be left the thickness of the finger and put another needle in that, and let it be tied with thread as it is tied with the first needle; and do not cease to do this if there is a need for needles until all the contraction of the plague is tied.
And let the needles be intermediate between thickness and subtlety, for subtle needles pierce easily into the flesh; and thick ones are difficult to insert into the cord, therefore let them be more subtle in thickness and grossness.
And let the needle not be put in two extremities of the cord that is near, lest the flesh be pierced deeply and the plague open before its consolidation.
And let them not be prolonged so that the plague is prohibited from consolidation.
And let the extremities of the needles be inserted so that they do not lead the patient out of his sleep; and pause over the stitching powdered herbs expanded from every part, which have the extremities of the needles; and let them remain until you know that the plague is consolidated.
And these spices of stitching with needles in cold manner are more convenient in small plagues, for they have in their stitching one needle or two or around them.
But the second stitching is common: and it is that you administer with stitching of the four extremities, of the cord and two extremities of the ciphac, in one go, with a needle in which there is thread wrapped in subtlety and thickness.
And let there be reduction of the needle from that penetrated party from which you began, so that the thread drawn back goes from the upper part to the plague, so that the stitching is according to stitching of cloth with which the clothes are tightened.
And pause between each stitch the amount of the thickness of a little finger.
And this suturing heals men.
And with such a suturing I healed a plague that had afflicted a man in his belly, and he had been plagued by it for seven days, and the rupture of the plague was about a palm's width, and it had come out around two palms from the intestine at the middle of the abdomen, and there was disruption in the middle of the abdomen; then I reduced it after the intestine had been torn open by the plague for 24 hours.
And the plague was consolidated within a space of 15 days or thereabouts, and I cured it, and he is healed; and then he lived for many years, and returned to his natural state.
And other doctors had judged that he would not be healed; and by God's miracle I cured him with ointment, for it was in a place where ointment and any medicine were ineffective, but I applied it to the plague twice daily, and washed it frequently with honey water until he was healed.
But I will tell of two kinds of suturing that are proper according to the narrative of the Word.
And you should begin with the thread's suture, and insert the needle from outside into inside at the beginning.
And when the needle penetrates the thread and the tissue according to the straightness and in the length of all the body, leave the end of the thread in which you have inserted the needle; and make the needle penetrate in the other end from inside to outside at the other end of the wound.
And when you have made that penetration, then do it again on the other side of that meteyssed end of the wound from outside to inside; and leave the end of the thread which is in this side, and make the needle penetrate at the other end from inside to outside.
And make that penetration with which you make that penetration in the wound' end in the middle of the abdomen, so that all that penetrates inside.
Then begin on this side of the wound and sew that with the end which is of the thread in the other side, and draw out the needle from the thread through which you have made penetration, and then reduce that to that thread and sew the end of the thread which is in the other side with this end of the abdomen; and draw out that from the thread on the part of him.
And do this once and again, and do this another time within that you sew all that is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this way of suturing is that you sew with a thread of skin, in such a way that you sew the abdomen one side from the end of the thread, and draw out that other side so that it is complete.
But the second kind of proper suturing which I narrate is from Gualia' s word, and it is of a man' s speech and by him recommended; it is that you sew that according to the similarity with which someone sews another thing, in such a way that they join all part to its comparable part conveniently by nature; and apply the end of one of his parts and the end of the wound to the other end of him, and that is done according to how I recommend to you.
Because you put the needle in the end of the wound near to the outside, and make it penetrate inside at that sole, and draw out two ends of the abdomen; then you revolve the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside in two ends of the abdomen.
Then revolve those again and make some of the ends penetrate from inside to outside in two ends of the wound that are in the contrary side.
And this way of suturing is better than the common simple suture, and it is the suture of which I preceded the memory.
This is Gualia' s word.
And others say, the suture of the abdomen is rectified with two needles; it is that you put in these some twine, and begin with penetration of the needle from what is below you, and make it penetrate inside at the other side according to the suturing of those who sew bodies together.
And know that when disruption is in the middle of the abdomen, then its suture is more difficult than of other places of the abdomen.
But the judgement of medicine for plague is the judgement of other plagues.
The cause of which is that when you know the plague recently with its blood before the air changes it, and reduce the intestine and sew it and adapt it decently, then pause over it powdery flesh-like matter.
And if the air has already changed the plague, then pause over it some ointments which are to you present so that they make hard stone and enclose the threads, and be consolidated abdomen and wound; and cure it thus as is the cure of other plagues so that it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines, then pause over it at once, for when it begins to fester, cut it much and change that twice a day, thus as you teach and instruct, until it is healed, for you do not need any other medicine in many conditions when the plagues are simple.
But if you fear that the plague communicates in damage to the principal members, then know that you submerge lion wool in oil of excellent quality, or in rose oil, and pause that in the circuit of the places that are between the angles and below the ankles.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in your intestines from how many times it should be, then baptize that with black astringent wine, and especially if the putrefaction is come by the intestine and there is made an exit through its cavity.
And know that that which is of the large intestine is a more difficult cure than that which is in the subtle intestines.
And it is of difficult cure because the intestine which is called "gegunu" does not receive the cure of plague that boxes in it from all; and that is because of the great multitude of veins that are in it, and because of its magnitude that is in those, and because of the subtlety of that one, and because of its proximity to nature of nerves.
But if that which proceeds from the plague is the membrane, and you obtain that recently, reduce that according to your straightness with which you reduce intestines equally.
But if it has passed to the space and is already made green or black, then you should tighten that with thread over the place that is blackened of it, for there to come flows of blood, for in the membrane there are veins and arteries.
And then insert that which is another one of it and pause two ends of the thread hanging from the part below the plague out of it, so that on you be elevated the attraction of it because of the weight of the membrane and emission of the pus of the plague.
Memory of the plagues when they happen in the intestine.
When disruption happens in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it be restored in some men, because I saw him who was plagued in his abdomen by blow of spear, and the plague was on the right part of the stomach, and the plague was old and made fistula, and exuded from it and ventosity.
Then I followed the cure of him so that he not be confident in the cure of it, but continued to subtilize that until it was healed and the place is consolidated.
And after I saw the site already consolidated, I applied medicine on the patient that had a bad accident in his belly; and he did not experience any discomfort from this bad accident, and he maintained his normal habits of eating and drinking, and also bathed and exercised as usual.
And some experienced people have said that when there is a small wound in the intestine, it should be treated as follows: you take a large leaf; and then you press the two lips of the wound and place one of those leaves which has an open mouth over the two lips of the wound; thus when it grabs onto those and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, because it will adhere and not come undone.
And then place another leaf near the first one, and do not cease to do this with leaves one after the other according to the size of the entire wound.
And then return that one and sew up the wound; and let those leaves remain attached to the intestine until it is healed, and do not harm the patient in any way.
And it is possible that the intestine be tied with a thin thread made from an animal's intestine, as it is easier to put on a needle; and you take one end of this intestinal thread, which is quite bare, and then tie the end of it with the thread, wrapping it around, and then insert that thread through needles and sew up with that the intestine, and return it to its cavity.
And this method of sewing with leaves is not certain but rather a matter of hope and expectation.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, then there is no other way but to cut it out, for there is no complete healing of it at all.
On the treatment of ulcers and fistulas:
Know that any wound and pustule which becomes inflamed and old, and is turned into an ulcer, and does not heal and keeps draining pus all the time and does not come to a head, then, in whatever part of the body it may be, universally it is named a fistula, and we call it by this name.
And a fistula according to the truth is a hollow nodule, hard, white, without pain and having a cavity like a hole of a bird; and because of this some call it a nodule, that is, a lump.
And in some men it is moist, and the moisture is discharged in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes scanty, and sometimes thin.
I have already shown all these causes, and their signs, and the treatment of them with medicines in that division.
Now let me inform you about the treatment of a fistula which is done by means of an operation with the hand.
Know that fistulas occur in all parts of the body: for some reach into large veins or arteries, or nerves, or the brain, or intestines, or bladder, or any of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the back, or into any of the joints composed by composition like that of the hand and foot; and some go near to the main part of a similar nature.
And therefore those of these fistulas which are in this way are among the most difficult diseases to heal, and do not receive treatment, and their occupation is labor and ignorance.
And if there is any fistula whose depth does not reach those places which I have mentioned, and you wait and trust in its healing, and wish to obtain knowledge of it, then take a needle or an awl, and if the fistula goes straight, probe it with that.
But if the fistula is winding, then investigate it with a probe made of thin lead, because lead, by the lightness of its body, is drawn away and bent according to the winding.
And if the fistula has horrifying depths, it is not possible for you to indicate it with a probe.
Then, Christianize the orifices by your orifices, for the moisture by which you Christianize it comes from the other orifices and flows from them.
And then investigate carefully, according to which way you are able to know whether there is a bone or nerve, and whether the fistula' s cavity is distant or near, or whether it is a fistula whose many orifices are above all by the use of your strength, by your investigation with the hand on the site, and like the signs and causes of the ulcer.
And when you have understood all these causes with certain knowledge, then go to the treatment according to confidence: and consider whether it is an apparent fistula near or in a salty place, distant from a joint, or of a nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of those sites which I have mentioned.
Then dry up the fistula according to what has been said about its origin, and stay that which is in that filtration and of rotten flesh, and that which is born in the orifice of flesh, or of thistle and like things, and heal that within as it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth and straight, then it is necessary that you probe it with an awl as far as it is possible for you to go, and then cleanse it of all its rotten flesh; and then administer hot poultices wrapped in sharp medicines, and insert those into the cavity of the fistula, which you have understood by means of the iron.
And do that some times within so that the sharp medicine corrodes all that is rotted in the cavity of the fistula from corruption.
And then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh within as it is healed.
But if it is not healed by that, then cauterize it according to what we have recommended.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and it is because you have certified that, then probe it and dry up that according to what we have recommended, unless you prohibit any cause of vein or nerve or main member as we have said.
But if the bone is revealed to you and there is corruption and blackness in it, then cut out that corruption entirely; and then heal it with that which will flesh it within as it is healed.
And if it is not healed and continues to discharge pus as before, then know that you have not attained the complete eradication of corruption; and then uncover it again and investigate it carefully and do the reason for it and the cleansing of it with your greatest strength, and then restore it.
And if it is healthy, then it is good; and if not, then know that this corruption is in the distant depths of the body, which has not been achieved, so you should not attempt to treat it.
If the bones are rotten or broken, and you can extract them with delicate forceps, then proceed to do so.
If they are loose bones, then carefully extract them all without leaving any trace behind with your strength.
But if you encounter any resistance from the moisture and find no fear or error in its source, then cut into those ligaments or meats as they are here, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones at this time, then place over the wound something that will fester and leave that wound open so that the flesh and ligaments surrounding the bones may suppurate, and keep the wound from healing so that the incision you have made in your operation is not closed.
However, if you are afraid of this, place over it a poultice soaked in vinegar, or a poultice soaked in Egyptian ointment, or one of the green ointments; for when those ligaments fester and the bones are separated from each other by suppuration, then their attraction is lessened.
But if it is a large bone like the shinbone or thighbone or one similar to those, and what is rotten in it is only its fascia, then cut away that corruption with the resection only as far back as the rottenness extends, and close up the wound.
But if what is rotten in it is a large portion of bone, and the corruption has already reached into the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close that up and cut away all around where the corruption is located, and then heal that wound so that it may be consolidated.
And know this: that you should apply something to the patient's thigh for a while as an example and aid in your operation.
A young man of about 30 years had pain in his thigh, and from the affected parts within was matter in the body which reached the thigh, and it was suppurative matter with large abscess, and there was not to him an external cause.
And so the time was prolonged by the error of the doctors until the abscess opened up, and many matters gathered there, and its cure was delayed because the thigh had been fistulated, and there were in him many orifices all of which discharged pus and the moisture of the body.
And so he was treated by many doctors for about two years, and there was no improvement but rather worsening in the leg where I had been called to treat him.
And so I saw his thigh and the matters that came from him through those orifices as a great flow; and indeed the man' s body was totally swollen and his color was yellowish.
And so I inserted the probe into one of those orifices and reached the bone.
And then I probed and searched thoroughly all the orifices and found that they led to all parts of the thigh.
And so I cut and opened up one of those orifices where I discovered a portion of the bone, and found it rotten, and indeed it was earthly and blackened, and a portion of the thigh had become rotten because it had penetrated into the membrane.
And then I cut away what was revealed to me and prepared from the uncovered bone.
And so I found that it was not in the bone but rather that corruption which I cut away and removed, and indeed I had already removed it.
And then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicine for a long time, and it was not consolidated.
And then I repeated this and uncovered the bone again on the second discovery, and found continued corruption with the bone.
And so I removed what appeared to me from that corruption, and then I found it necessary to do the cure of it and restoration, and it was not restored.
And so I uncovered it again and did not cease to probe the bone little by little particularly and perforate the restoration of it, and it was not restored until I had probed around an inch of it, and I extracted that with its marrow, and then I restored it with medicines, and it was more consolidated and healed.
And it was not necessary to repeat the operation on him or to cut him again except according to the disposition of his condition and due to the weakness of his body and his fear of death, for he suffered in all hours from excess of discharge and a bad syncope.
And so he is healed entirely, and there is nothing in the place of hard bone; and his disposition is good in his body and he has recovered his strength; and he is returned to his former dispositions and did not encounter any difficulty in walking or damage from any cause.
But if the bone is protruding from the body and has been cut, then you should proceed thus: take ligament and strengthen that at the extremity of that prominent bone, and command someone to stretch that while you apply the other ligament of wool which is thicker over the meat that is below the bone, and extend the extremity of it so that you pull the meat away from the parts below, and uncover the meat over the place where you want to cut so that the saw does not damage the meat.
And place a post or board below the bone decently, for when you do this it is not prohibited that the saw cuts into the rotten meat.
And know that the cutting is on the rotten place little by little, for if it is in the concavity of the bone and does not appear to the sense, then force the saw another time.
And if the bone is rotten and not prominent, then the one part is continued with the other, and the corruption is in the middle or in a part of it, then uncover the meat from all parts entirely; and then place a post below, and then cut that from the first part where the corruption is so that the sawing continues to the other part.
And let the sawing be according to the place of the corruption a little, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, insert that corrupt joint, and cut the bone where it continues to the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no ratio but skill.
And if the corruption is in the breast of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however know that you probe the corruption in whatever way it appears to you, and open that up according to each disposition which is possible for you, and with each skill be directed to you, when it does not go against you vein or nerve.
And know which are the saws and the probes to cut these bones according to their situation, and the preparation of them, and their thickness, and subtlety and greatness, and smallness, and hardness, and softness; so that you may be prepared for all kinds of operations conveniently for that operation.
And know that this operation indicates to you about the kind of instrument of which you have experience; when with you is great study and long and understanding of the ways of this art, and testimony of ways of diseases, for then you already had through your masters what is convenient to them of instruments to every disease.
