Chapter One:
Summary and collection of the things that must be known beforehand about fractures of bones, from which precedes the discussion of the healing of broken members, each according to its kind. Let us therefore take up in the proemium of this chapter those things pertaining to speech, and those chapters that are necessary for understanding the truth about these matters; you and those who are skilled in this noble art will be able to understand them promptly, as well as others who may be learning it.
And so I say that when a fracture or dislocation or twisting or displacement occurs, you must first consider using cauterization, or bandaging it, or any other treatment, unless some other condition prohibits it, as well as considering the debility of the patient's strength, or whether the injury is to an infant, or an elderly person, or whether it happens during a period of intense heat or intense cold.
Then the patient should eat a diet of fresh herbs and bird flesh and crab meat; and he should abstain from fatty meats and starchy foods, and from all foods that fill the veins with blood, as long as you can be sure that there will be no pus forming in cauterized wounds, and unless you fear the effusion of matter into the affected limb.
Then return the patient to his usual diet, from which he deviated because of his illness.
And so when the broken bone begins to heal, then let the patient be fed with nourishing and substantial foods that are moist and heavy, such as those made from wheat flour cooked in water, rice, peas, beef, mutton, chicken, fresh fish, wine, and similar drinks; and by following this regimen, the fracture will heal sooner and better.
And know that the bones of old people and children will never completely heal and be consolidated according to their nature due to the drying out of their limbs.
But Nature adjusts and brings together the broken bone from every side by a kind of glue that adheres and binds it together until it reaches the end of its strength and firmness, just as fire does at first, unless some condition prohibits it from doing so.
And for this reason, it is necessary to feed the patient foods that are heavy and viscous and thick, as we have said.
And know that the kinds of fractures are different according to the difference in limbs, for a fracture of the bone in the leg is different from a fracture of the bone in the head; and a fracture of the bone in the foot is different from a fracture of the bone in the hand; and likewise all the other limbs differ among themselves.
And I have covered the recollection of every kind of fracture, expounded in its own distinct chapter from the others.
There is a difference among fractures according to where they occur. And the kinds of fractures of bones differ according to their locations; for some occur in an arched manner and others in stars; and there are fractures according to the length of the bone, such as those that happen in the fingers, and these are the fractures of the metatarsus and phalanges, whether proceeding or not proceeding from another, just as they are when writing is prepared on a pen, and there are fractures with splitting and disruption in the head, and fissure-like fractures.
And each kind has its own inherent pain or torment during healing according to the location that will be recounted later, distinct from others in their locations.
There is a sign of bone fracture. And by this is bone fracture known; there is crookedness of it, and an appearance and sensation of it to the touch, and also when you press on it with your hand.
And therefore when there is no crookedness in the location nor an appearance or sensation when you touch it with your hand and the patient does not feel great pain when you press on it, then there is no fracture.
But it is possible that there is crookedness or a small fissure, and yet you do not feel pain when you touch it with your hand nor when you press on it; then pause over it the medicines about which I will make mention later at this moment, and appropriate for that location; and let there be tightness by subtle compression.
And know that the location when it is arched and not split into two or otherwise that has occurred in the metatarsus, as a whole part of its bone is torn off, then you must begin to straighten it by cauterizing the wound; then let there be strictness by subtle compression.
And if it has cauterized the wound, then leave off that for several days until the cautery stops; and appropriate according to whatever manner you can lift it up and move it around.
And know that the healing of it and its setting is more gradual than in the bone where the metatarsus occurred.
And let there be tightness according to what will be recounted later.
But if in the bone of the metatarsus, then extension of this broken limb from two parts is necessary, whether it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two strings or one string and the hand.
And let your position be such that you hold the limb in its natural posture while the parts are extended from the broken limb, then press that you may force them to return to their original state and according to whatever manner you can in their locations.
And beware according to your strength that you do not apply too much force over the patient through your operation of them nor harm him; and press according to your power that you apply one bone against another according to their various forms.
And let you touch it with your hand in that hour profitably and if you see any diverse cause, straighten it and equalize it according to the amount of your strength.
And avoid violent extension and compression, just as some foolish doctors cause cauterization of the wound through their operation or cause contraction in the limb just as we have seen this happen through their operation many times.
And the quality of the constriction of the limb is that which I have described, so that you may know that broken limbs are distinguished by their smallness and their bigness and the shape of them.
And those that are small like the arm and the elbow and the wrist, let the wrapping be of a very thin cloth.
But those that are large like the thigh or the leg, let the wrapping be of hard, leaden cloth, for the tight binding of the heavy cloth connects and strengthens the large limb all around, so that no mark will enter it.
Then, after your own evacuation, put a convenient bandage on it over the site of that fracture with three or four wrappings according to how the limb deserves it; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
Then go to that at the highest point of the site of the fracture and tighten so that your tightening with which you have tightened the site of the fracture is not slackened. Then loosen it a little with the wrapping at the site of the fracture and loosen the tightness little by little as you take a large part from the site.
Then take another bandage and wrap the other side of the broken site with it, then go to the bottom part of the bandages at the fracture; and your operation should be in tightening the bandage and loosening it little by little as we said above about the first bandage.
Then place between the bandages a piece of cloth or a bandage with which the crookedness of the fracture is equalized if there is crookedness; but if not, then do not put anything there for any reason.
Then put another bandage over that and tighten it over these decently-wrapped bandages as soon as possible if there is no inflammation or abscess at the site, but if there is, then put on what stops the abscess and eliminates the inflammation; leave it for a few days, and then you can tighten the bandages.
And the bandages should be made of thin leaded cloth and skillfully formed, or of wood, or of palm-tree bark; and each bandage's width should be according to that shape and form.
However, the bandage that is put on at the fracture should be thicker and a little harder than the others; and the length of it according to how long the limb is in size and smallness. And tighten above it with another bandage as we said about tightening, and tie above it decently as we said about tying, and let your tightening be according to the site of the fracture more, and as much as the fracture is farther away from the fracture, the less the tightness should be.
And the threads should be medium-sized in thickness and subtlety, and they should be made of strong linen; and if the threads are thick, then as I have seen some foolish people doing their operations by tying their threads with rope, there is a great error when the tightness is not equal.
And thin threads are not convenient, for you will not achieve through tightness with them what you want, and there should not be more than a finger's width between bandages.
And if the patient is hurt by the ends of the bandages when they are tight on the limbs, then put underneath them lead or wool that has been smeared with something to prevent injury.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a plague and disruption in the tendon, and I have come to remember that one in the chapter according to its uniqueness.
And know that not every broken limb is tight with bandages on the first day; the cause of this is because when it is large, you should not put bandages over it except after a few days or seven or more, according to your confidence about the occurrence of an abscess.
And this is the manner of the plasters with which the Ancients restored, which were put on fractures and dislocations and twists.
Manner of plaster as a means of restoring fractures, suitable for many complications and especially for women and children, because it is that which does not come heat or rub: and you take from the powder of milling, and it is the meal's pith that comes out with the motion of the mill on the walls of the mill.
And prepare that as it is, unless you sieve it with egg white, and do not make the preparation thick or subtle; then use that.
Another method of restorative ointment for fracture, dislocation and twisting: take assafetida, myrrh, acacia, enula, and mastic which is made from wild mastic trees, of all of these ingredients take one drachm each; of myrrh and aloe, take five drachms each; of garlic juice, take twenty drachms; mix them all together with honey water or with egg white if the patient's complexion is scorched; and use this ointment, for it is of good composition and quickly restores broken bones, and benefits many people.
Another method of ointment which benefits fractures and twisting: take mastic and white madder, both one drachm each; myrrh and aloe, five drachms each; acacia, six drachms; balsam of Armenia, twenty drachms.
All of these are to be pounded in a mortar with a pestle, and sifted, and mixed with water or egg white, and administered.
Manner of plaster for joints and bones separated from their sockets, and sore pain accidentally in those, and also for splits and fractures: take wool that is plucked and let it be soaked in wine that has been cut with oil, and let it be put on the site.
But in this plaster there is not virtue of restoration, nor of an abscess, but it eliminates pains properly.
Another manner of plaster which restores a broken bone: take figs' leaves and wild poppy leaves; pound them all together and make a plaster from them.
Manner of plaster applied after the restoration of a bone fracture and when you want resolution of the residue of an abscess: take pounded white poplar bark, camomille and violets' flowers, and bran, all pounded .i. parts. All of these are to be pounded and soaked in wine if the limb is not scorched.
But if it is scorched, then prepare that with water of henna or with water and use it.
Another stronger plaster in resolution than that first one, when an abscess occurs after the restoration of a limb: take pounded white poplar bark and linseed, and myrrh, marjoram, violets' flowers, camomille, all pounded i alike; all of these are to be pounded and soaked in water according to the quality of the limb and its rest.
But Ipocras does not dry in his own juice when it is put on a broken limb after its restoration unless it is made of wax and oil and nothing else; and let it be thinner between thickness and thinness.
But it has been seen that they are put on a broken limb after its restoration and the causes in which there is dryness with little heat are like myrrh, anise and similar to those causes that we have mentioned.
A method of plaster that confers ease and relief from injury and pain: take a mixture of honey, sesame seeds, human hair or bird feathers, and aloe, and salt, all ground to a powder; strain and sift them, and mold them into a paste, and make a plaster.
However, the amount of time it takes for the healing process to complete will vary depending on how severe the injury is; so you must consider whether there is still pain or swelling or if the bone is broken or displaced. If not, then do not remove the plaster for several days. And if there is intense pain or swelling or inflammation in the area, then you must cut or remove the plaster from it immediately.
And take a piece of linen and marine sponge, wet it in tepid water, and with that wash the area until the swelling and pain are reduced; then let the limb rest for an hour; then apply lanolin saturated with sweet wine or oil, and leave it on overnight so that the wound is protected from infection and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain is relieved.
And then repeat this gentle compression with a small plaster, but do not tighten it too much with your initial binding; do this gently so that it heals.
And if all the swelling and heat and pain and inflammation and all the other symptoms have subsided completely, and you no longer need plasters or bandages, then repeat what you did at the beginning in the same way.
And if none of the aforementioned causes occur in the limb, then do not remove it but rather leave it on for three or four or five or seven days. And sometimes it is left on for twenty days, depending on how well the limb responds to having the plaster removed, as we have said, so that the fracture can heal and the flesh can adhere to it.
And then tighten the bandage more than your initial bandaging, according to how well the patient remembers it, and adjust the padding around the patient,
But if you see that the fracture is dried out and weakened more than it should be, then know that nutrition is prohibited from reaching it; so soak it with tepid water every day for three days; loosen the bandage a little, and through this operation, nourish the limb, and it will heal quickly.
But what fools do when they restore a broken limb is another way when it is not restored at the beginning as it should be, and it is restored according to crookedness, is an error according to their operation, and great harm; and if this were known to the Ancients in their books, they would have used it.
And I have not seen any of them do this, so it is more reasonable that it not be done.
Chapter 2: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and the shapes of them are diverse, and the causes are distinct; for some fractures are made by the impact of a club, and the impact strikes all the bone within the skull, which is below the brain, just as it happens in an axe, and through this that type of fracture is named concussion; or the club strikes a part of the bone and penetrates into the flesh but does not go beyond its outer edge; and this type of fracture is named absolute elevation.
And the wound of these two fractures may be large or small, and they are either compressive or tensile; and the cause of them is impact with a fist or a hard object on stone, and similar objects.
And this fracture is either penetrating that it approaches the membrane which is below the bone, or in the surface of the bone, and the wound of these two fractures may be large or small.
And another fracture is when it is hidden in the bone, in the sottlety of the hair, and through this that type of fracture is named capillary fracture.
And another fracture is made either by impact or by the blow of a stone and similar objects, causing one of the bone's thin layers to enter into its hollows; and this occurs in the fingers when they are hit with a blow and are split from within.
And it most often occurs in the bodies of wet nurses, just as their breasts' nipples crack when they wean infants.
And all these types of fractures are bloodless, just as they are separated by membranes. And I will come with the memory of all this in my mind.
And we know all these types of fractures by their detection over there and by the inquiry into them through touch and the appearance of corrupted flesh that is over them.
And capillary fracture is known by what appears after it is uncovered on the bone; when it is wiped and oiled, for the fracture then appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures, you must first consider the patient's conditions; and then if you see through these conditions that something signifies by manifest indication regarding fear, just as it happens with vomiting in cholera, and extension, and loss of understanding, and lack of speech, and fainting, and acute fever, and heat and redness in those, and appearance like theirs in the more serious dispositions, then it will not heal.
And if you see that these conditions are not present, then you may begin its treatment.
The reason for this is that if the wound comes to you in the beginning as it is wounded, and it is in the winter days, then you must study the appearance of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every disposition.
And if it is in the summer days, then you must cut off the bone before the seventh day, having seen that what is below the membrane is corrupted, and that the conditions we have recounted occur.
And if the fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain and is in small pieces with tension, then you must insert the fractured piece, according to what I will recount to you.
And it is that you cut open the head of the patient who has been wounded, and uncover the bone according to custom and method known to you according to the appearance of the wound, and let it be easy on the patient.
And if it happens to you that you must make the detection of the bone, and there is blood or pus, then you must stop it with what comes over it, and it is that you apply a poultice of cloths soaked in wine and oil to the wound, and let it rest so that the pus is reduced; and then begin the circuit of the bone and its removal.
And it shall be according to one of two methods of operation.
And one way is that you insert the bone with a tight and narrow chisel, and this is its shape: And then administer another chisel, a little wider than the first; and this is its shape:
And then apply the chisel wider than the first.
And it must be that you have a number of various chisels, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are sharper than others and are in the shape of aiguilles, and they are of iron or steel.
And you must use them with ease when hitting the incision so that it does not bruise the head or lead to that.
And if the bone is strong and hard, then you must drill through it before administering the incisions with the chisels, which are called non-penetrating chisels.
And they are not called that because they do not reach the end of the bone but remain above it; for the chisel is round at its tip or edge below the sharp point, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take many of these chisels, each suited to the thickness of a different bone, while keeping in mind, in all parts of the skull, that its extremity should be sharp, and its quantity in length and breadth should be according to the quantity of thickness of that part.
And this is the form of three kinds of chisels: The quality of drilling through the broken bone is that you place the chisel on the bone and turn it with your fingers while knowing that the bone has been penetrated; then change the chisel to another place, and place what is between all traces of the quantity of thickness of the thread or around that.
Then insert with chisels what is between each crack of the bone, and make that as subtle as you can, in the end of lightness, thus as we have said, while you radiate the bone, or with your hand, or by some other means for which you have prepared tools, such as forceps and subtle tongs.
And you must be careful that the chisels do not touch any part of the brain.
And when you have drilled through the bone and separated it from the brain if it is still attached to it, and have extracted that, then scrape and gnaw at that in the asperity of it with another tool resembling a chisel, but however make sure that it is more thin and more subtle than the other chisels.
And if any small bones and fragments remain, then pick those up carefully with the tools you have prepared for that purpose; then cure the wound with poultices and ointments as we have said.
And the other manner of operation is lighter and more distant from fear, which Guiana praises and washes with great praise; and this is its speech: it is necessary for you at the beginning to begin to expose the part of the bone in the location in which there is the most violent and most manifest fracture, while when you have exposed that part and placed below it the extremity of this chisel, slow and gentle, of which this is the form:
And the lenticular part is lion-like and does not enter anything, and the acute part is of it in two sides in length, thus as a wedge; let the lenticular part be applied to the brain with the acute face of it drilling into the bone.
Then strike upon the chisel with a small mallet while you drill into all of the bone with ease as it is turned, and be secure about the membrane not happening to him accidentally in any manner whatsoever, and if the patient was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were sleeping.
Then if there remains any cause attached to the bone of the membrane in some places of the bone, cut that off from it with the extremity of that lenticular chisel, and deliver him from it with ease, for it is delivered from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the membrane, and that which is cut off or remains of the bone is its face, and there remain in it asperity and subtle fragments, then scrape that asperity and pluck those fragments with subtle razors for which you have prepared a number, of diverse quantities so that it may be possible for you to administer those most conveniently in every place and most rightly according to where you wander in your operation on that broken bone.
And it is convenient that you apply in your reason with which you scrape the bone at the beginning the most edge of those razors, and then use that most subtilely, and do not cease doing that according to continuity until you come to administer the most thin and the most subtle of all those.
But in any of the other little hairline fractures and subtle fractures, let you apply cure according to what you have wandered into its correction.
And it is a cause that is not diminished over all those who have studied in that art. And we have written about what we have shown in great fractures.
And if the membrane is stretched when you drill into the bone, then take cloth according to the quantity of the poultice, and soak that in rosewater, and place that over the orifice of the poultice; then take another cloth folded or doubled and soak it in wine and rosewater and place that over the first cloth and do that as gently as you can so that you do not weigh down the brain; then apply to that liniment, and do not strain that except for the quantity that the poultice has only as much.
And leave that with this cure for a day or two until you are sure of cauterized pus; then dislodge that and then administer medicines with which there is superfluous dryness, such as linseed oil and bran, and powder of onions and of aristology, and of similar things to those.
And it is that you make of these medicines powder which is powdered over the poultice, thus as they are dried; and universally, apply to that all medicine of a property of which there is that it cleanses and does not bite.
And be careful according to your skill that the poultice in your cure is clean; and there is not in it thickness of ointment or other; nor do you leave virulence in it; for when it is applied over the membrane of the brain, it corrupts that and injures that, and there happens about the patient an evil malicious malice.
And it injures the brain of the head when the bone is cleansed and chiefly when the cure of that is diminished, and there is blackness on the surface of it; then consider therefore if there happen to the patient the accidents which we have said, know that there are dangerous omens in all directions.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine that is placed above it, and there is in virtue of that medicine that makes that blackness, then it is convenient that you take a portion of honey and portions .iii. of rosewater and with those lines a cloth, and then place that over the membrane; then cure that with the spices of cure which are convenient so that it is healed.
Chapter .iii.
Of restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two parts altogether most sovereign, or one of those some few times, because in it are two bones, for the part below is cartilage, and is not broken, and nothing happens to it except compression and crookedness and narrowness.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then it is convenient that you put your finger in your nose and gnash that fracture between with your index finger and with your thumb outside, while you return the nose according to its natural figure. And let that be done by you with ease, and be careful that it does not bruise the patient through that your operation.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose, and the bone does not project out to that point, then it should be made even with the extremity of the bone in which there is a small protuberance.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you should make it like that.
And apply the plaster on him in the first day of the fracture if it is possible; and if not, then let him have the warm bath or the hot bath with the rest of the poultice cauterized.
Then insert into the extremity of the nose a piece of linen if there is one fracture, or put two splints if there are two fractures; and let the splints have thickness of such quantity that it fills up the hole in the nose.
And some of the old healers say that the splints be dipped in wax, and changed every day, but I have not seen it; it is better that the piece of linen is dipped in egg white cooked with honey like powder; then let the splint be placed inside so that the bone is secured and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes instead of the splints, put in the nose a feather of an owl wrapped with linen; and let there be the most violent fracture of the nose and that the patient is not prohibited from inspiration.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want, do it, and if you want, do splints.
And if another poultice cautery happens again, plaster the nose with cerotom or with cotton dipped in sweet wine and rose oil, or with daryol.
But if the poultice cautery does not happen, then let you plaster that defect with sesame flour and powder of anise cooked with egg white; and place on it a piece of linen, and do not touch the nose for any reason.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are crushed, then let you split them and extract them with instruments that are convenient for them; and close the split and cure it with something that carnifies and consolidates, and plaster them with something convenient.
And if there is pus inside the nose, then let you cure it with splints and administer lead tubes so that it is completely healed.
Chapter .iiii.
Of restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated.
When the jaw is broken and there is no pus in it, then examine if the fracture is only outward and not broken into two and bent inward in its parts, then its understanding is easy.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, then you insert your left index finger in the patient's mouth; and likewise if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert that left index finger; and displace with that the displacement of the fracture inside with ease to its parts outward with your other hand on the outward part of the bone; with that decently equal it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then let you administer extension of its two parts according to straightness so that its equalization is possible.
And if there is commotion of teeth to disruption, then strengthen those from which you expect that they will break with thread of gold or silver or silk; and plaster on the broken jaw cerotom, and place over it doubled linen, and place on the linen a great star decently made, or a weight of only lead at the length of the jaw; and tie that above according to how the fastening of it is equipped to you and convenient to its application, so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient that he uses rest and tranquility, and feed him with a soft diet.
And if you think that the figure is already altered in some way, then you untie and loosen it on the third day; and correct what has already been altered from him and plaster him with powder of anise, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after having taken off his plaster, and place over the plaster linen wool.
And once you have stood and tightened that plaster over it, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it, so that you do not loosen it until it is healed and the fracture is strengthened; for many times this fracture is tightened in three weeks.
And if another poultice cautery happens again, then administer what we have said many times in the healing of it so that the pus is evacuated from it.
And if the fracture is with pus, then consider whether the bone mass is separated or shattered; then you subtilize and remove those shatters with what is convenient for it in the removal of them with instruments.
And if the hole of the pus is narrow, then you amplify it with phlebotomy according to the quantity of your need.
And when you have removed those shatters and from them no pus has been evacuated, then close the hole of the pus if it is wide; and if not, place over it one of the plasters that are convenient for it and carnify it, so that it is healed.
Chapter .v.
Of restoration of the collarbone when it is dislocated.
Many times the collarbone is broken from in front of the muscle, and that fracture is done in one of three ways.
For it is broken or separated, or is separated into two parts other than the shatters do not happen; and its restoration is easy; or it happens that the fracture is with pus, and its restoration is difficult; or the fracture is with pus.
And the operation in it, when the fracture is with pus, is that you have two ministers present, so that one has an assistant and follows the collarbone broken, and the other extends its collar to that broken part.
Then join the fracture with your fingers so that it is made in its form according to how it should be, and do not leave in it a little prominence or concavity.
And if you need more extension, then let you place below the chin of the patient a pillow of linen or wool, and let the size of it be according to the quantity of your need; and extend and raise the collarbone and contract the pillow with your hand so that you join the fracture according to how it should be.
And if you cannot bring the extremity of the collarbone to its parts outward because it is reached at the breast, then let the patient recline on his neck, and place over his chest a pillow on which he leans with his right shoulder, and place on his left shoulder a pillow in size.
And the minister presses the muscle of it to its parts downward so that the bone of the collarbone is elevated that it is in the breast below, and then you correct the fracture and join it with your fingers.
But if you feel shatters of the collarbone and swollen, then let you split over it and evacuate those shatters with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you make an incision of it with one of the knives which you have equipped for it, after having placed below the collarbone the instrument that serves the saw; and the instrument is of wood or of iron of which this is its form:
And it is similar to an awl of which this is the concavity; and let its length be according to what you need from it, according to the size of the bone or the weakness of it.
May the length of the patient be according to what is possible for the operation; let there be two extremities as well as times, and one extremity be ample and the other tight.
And if the wound you have cut is wide after you have extracted the dead bone tissue, and it is certain that pus will collect, then you should close the two lips of the wound with a stitch.
And if the wound is small, or you fear pus will collect, then fill the wound with cotton and powdered drugs according to the amount of the cut wound.
And if pus does occur, then moisten a cloth in warm wine, rose oil and wine, and place it over the area.
And all of this should not be applied to the broken bone; and so that you can see the patient every day, if the binding becomes loose or you see the rod has become loose, then correct it and tighten the binding.
And then place the patient's pillow under his neck, and place another small cushion under his chest when he lies down at night so that with that his helper on that side is elevated, and therefore the broken clavicle is elevated due to the elevation of the muscle; or his arm is bent around his neck.
And don't loosen the binding unless pus occurs in the spot or it becomes swollen, for up to twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen his face turn that way, and adjust it in the constriction and restore him and leave it be until it is restored and the clavicle fracture has coagulated.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the moon in 28 days, and in some people it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Of the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The spatula is broken on the lateral side and not from him but from the margins.
And therefore when it is broken — and it is broken in the middle of him — and it is not known except by touch, then according to how the fracture is spread out you prepare it and reduce it according to its natural figure in every way that is possible for you.
And then place over the spot powdered wheat flour with egg white and lion's tooth; and then sprinkle cloth powder doubled over it; and then place over him a thin wooden rod according to the amount of the spatula or more.
But if under the rod there is a concavity in any of the places of the spatula, then you fill that concavity with lion's tooth, and then push the rod according to equality.
And then strengthen it above with a long ice pack with decent constriction and the firmness of the rod so that it is not loosened from its place.
And consider the binding every day; and if it is loosened, then bind another looseness and tighten it and adjust the rod if it has loosened from its place.
And keep the patient on his right side.
And the spatula is restored in 20 days or in .XXV.
And therefore when that number is completed, then you dissolve the binding, and know that of those bones whose beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if the spot of the bone weighs down and puts pressure underneath the collarbone, then you cut it there, and stop it, and act according to what we have said in the clavicle fracture about pus collection if it occurs here.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of a leg fracture.
The leg sometimes breaks in the middle and that is usually how it ends up; but its ends are very often crushed and broken.
And of its accidents when it is broken in the middle there is decline to the inside parts and occurs in him great pain, and difficulty in walking, and cough, and strong fever and vomiting of blood; and it is declared in the cavity in the broken bone and is not hidden from sensation.
And its restoration is that the patient is laid on his back, and a cushion placed between his shoulders, and then press against his muscles, and adjust his ribs with two hands, and act subtly in his operation and according to all ways that are possible for you and convenient to him, until he returns the bone' s figure according to how it should be.
And then place over him plaster and a towel, and place under it a thin wooden rod of ash or linden or similar in thickness, which you wrap in cloth; and then act subtly on it over the broken bone so that it is not loosened; and go around him with binding according to roundness at his back sometimes, and tighten it with decent constriction; and consider the binding at all times, and when it is loosened, tighten it.
And if necessity requires that it be loosened because of swelling or pain, or pus occurs in the spot, then you cut and loosen it and eradicate the plaster and correct what occurs to him by way of his cure; and then tighten the binding, if it occurs, according to his face; and adjust the constriction so that he is healed.
Chapter 8.
Of the restoration of ribs when they are cracked.
Know that rib fractures do not occur except in large places that follow the chest; and of their ends from the front part, no contriction occurs to them except by that which they are cartilaginous. And its sensation is not dulled in feeling because of the inquiry into it with fingers.
And its restoration is that you join the fracture with fingers according to the possible way, so that it is swallowed according to how it should be, and then plaster and tighten the broken bone with a rod if it occurs.
And if the rib fracture inclines to the inside parts, then occurs to him violent pain and piercing, as well as piercing that occurs to him who has wept, and because of it that puts difficulty in breathing, and cough, and vomiting of much blood; and this is of difficult cure.
And now the Antychus are born in him with many warts.
And from them you will say: let the patient who engenders inflammation and swelling abstain from foods that cause flatulence and distention, so that his belly is distended and the fracture is driven to the inside parts.
But we abstain from it so that it is not the cause of producing pus collection, if it has not occurred; and if it has already occurred, then help it.
And some of them say that it should be stopped above the vent and then sucked with strength; and it seems like a good idea, but it is feared that the wind may push out the superfluities to the location because of the weakness.
And some of them say: that the location should be covered with wool that is already dipped in hot oil, and there should be powder placed between the ribs where it is filled, so that the binding is equal to the roundness; and then the patient should be treated with a pleurisy poultice of food and medicine.
And if there is violent constriction that the patient cannot tolerate, and the bones puncture the membrane with their points, we fear for the patient; then we open the location above the broken rib, uncover it, and then place an instrument that conserves the pharynx on top of it, whose memory is preceded by incision of the bone with ease, and extract it.
And then we should sew both lips of the wound, if it is large, with a stitch, and treat it with poultices until it is healed.
And if blisters arise inside, then you cut and pour powder into rose oil and place it on the location, and the patient should be treated with what is appropriate for the blister inside.
And it should be re-bandaged on the side on which he sleeps, so that it may heal.
Chapter 9.
Of the restoration of a broken spine and collarbone.
Sometimes there is fracture of the bones of the neck, and they hurt in their traction, and similarly the vertebrae of the spine. And when it happens to someone and you want to know if it will heal or not, then consider that if you see the fingers soft, numb, and dead, and they cannot move those or stretch them, and when you press on them with your fingernails or puncture them with a needle and there is no pain in them, then you will know that it will not heal, and in most cases, this is fatal.
And if there is constriction in them with the fingers and puncture, then you will know that the bone's neck is saved and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see the vertebrae of the collarbone like this and want to know if it will heal or not, then consider his feet.
Now if you see that they are already soft and what happens to the fingers happens in his feet, and when he lies on his back, he has wind and involuntary defecation, and sometimes he is re-bandaged on his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then you will know it is fatal, so do not treat him.
But if none of those happen, the cause is better and it will heal more quickly.
And the treatment for him is to pierce the blisters with a needle and pour into them rose oil only, or with mallow leaves; place it on the broken vertebra some time during the day when the blister is settled.
Then place one of the comforting and soothing poultices on it and strengthen it with a bandage; and command the patient to take rest and tranquility, and not to sleep on the side that gives him pain until he is healed.
And if it happens because of the traction of the bone, or for some other reason that it is already separated from him, then dry over the wound and tie that bone; then sew both lips of the wound if it is large, and treat it with some ointment of the gums, so that it may heal.
But if the end of the bone of the hip is broken and it is the end of the socket, then you should insert your left finger into the anus, and grip the broken bone with your other hand as much as possible by second force; then place a poultice on him and splint him, if he has indigestion from them, and strengthen it.
And if you feel broken splinters in him, then open that and tie it up, and treat the wound with the treatment that is preceded so that it may heal.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the shoulder.
The bones of the shoulders are usually broken, and if they are broken, then their fracture is not but communication in their extremities and they are inserted into the length and decline to the parts within; and it happens to the patient that there is pain in the location and puncturing, and the arm is numbed when it is broken.
And its restoration is that you extend your hand over it so that it is above the fracture which is the figure of it.
And if the fracture is only in its extremity, then grip it according to how it appears to you by second force so that it is assembled into its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the length and inclines to the parts below, then you make the patient lie on his belly so that you may prepare for its restoration. And when you have gripped it, place a poultice on it, and then place a splint of wood or of cord on it, and strengthen it by compression over which you should not fear puncturing of the fracture nor removal of the splint.
And grip the hollow with whatever fills it so that it takes the compression according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on his back or on his side.
But if blister arises, then cease from extension of it and its restoration until the blister is settled, and place on it what settles that according to what precedes; then return to its restoration, and strengthen it thus as well.
And if they happen in the bones or are crushed, then you treat them.
Chapter 11.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the helper.
The helper is what is between the neck and goes to the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which the first is that you take wood of sea pine according to this form:
And let them be tied at both ends with two cords, and then it should be suspended from the raised location.
And let the patient lean on a crutch, and then bend his broken arm outward over the wood so that it reaches the tip of him in the middle location to which the curve of the wood is attached.
And then let him lean on it for some reason heavy, or the servant extend that to the parts below; and then the bone grip with their fingers, so that the fracture returns as it should.
And the other way is that the patient lies on his neck and suspends the hand of his collarbone with a bandage, and then command two servants so that one of them holds what is above the fracture with their fingers, and the other holds the feet below him; and each of them extends to his party.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then strengthen over the fracture with a bandage and over that with another bandage, and each of the two servants extends to their two parties.
And if the fracture is close to the extremity of the muscle, then let half of the bandage be placed over the tip and the other below the fracture, near the neck.
And similarly if the fracture is close to the collarbone, then let the bandage be placed over that location and over the collarbone.
Swallow it easily and without effort, and then when the throat is swallowed according to how each person is, and is applied with a good application, then strengthen it if an abscess does not occur.
But if an abscess occurs, then loosen the stricture within seven days, and place on it soft wool soaked in warm vinegar and rose water until the abscess heals, and then you strengthen it.
And the way to strengthen it is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a new cloth wrap over the plaster; and then adjust the arm to that support, and place your open hand over his muscles.
And place the cloth and strengthen it over the support of the arm so that the arm is in its place unless prohibited by any cause of the broken bone.
And then use splints.
And it is that you place over the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break with an addition of three fingers all around.
And then strengthen the splints with the stricture which was described at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your stricture over the site of the fracture be tighter. And when the fracture has healed, let the stricture be less.
And so if you see the arrangement of the splints and the stricture as we have said in the time in which you are restoring the limb to its setting, then you do.
And if you fear an abscess will form, then you loosen the stricture and the splints within seven days as we have said.
And then consider the ligament in all parts for three days so that no inflammation or swelling occurs at the site, or food is prohibited from coming to the limb due to the disposition of excess stricture; and then rectify all this as we have said.
But if you are certain that none of this will occur, then do not loosen the ligament but wait three days or more.
And let his sleep be on his back, and let his hands be over his stomach.
And place over the support a full poultice of wool which is moistened, and consider throughout the night or day that it not be loosed nor dissolved the figure of the broken limb, or that the ligament be softened, therefore rectify all this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have given before, so that the food is subtle at the beginning until it is strengthened; and then increase the food for him.
For the custom of the support and his bed is that they are tightened for 40 days, and then let them be loosed and a bath administered to him and he is medicated with ointments which rectify this.
And if the fracture is dry, then you do not loosen from it the ligament and splints within two months or 60 days.
Chapter .xii.
Of restoration of a broken arm.
An arm is made of two bones which are called two ulnas; of which the one is small and is that which supports the little finger, and the other is large and is that which is placed below the small one, from the part more below.
And often only the larger ulna is broken, or the smaller, and often they are both broken together.
And when the smaller ulna is broken above, then its restoration is easier, and its healing is even easier.
And when it is broken below the fracture is difficult to restore, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than this is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore if the bone which is broken is the smaller ulna above, then it is convenient for the doctor, during its restoration, to place a small extension with ease so that it be moistened.
But if the larger ulna is broken, then let him place an extension more vehemently.
And if both ulnas are broken together, then let you place the extension even stronger.
And let you place the figure of the hand in its restoration and extension stretched out over a pillow, and let the wrist of the hand be above the parts more up, more than all the other fingers, and let the auricular finger be more below than all the others, and let the patient be lying on one side.
And let one pillow be under him in the height or bend of him, so that he does not fall to one side.
And then the servant extends the arm from below with his own hand or with a ligament, and another servant extends above similarly.
And then the doctor moistens the bone so that he reduces it according to the best figure that he can.
And if in the setting of the bone there are bumps, then pierce through all the bumps at its site with your skill.
And if a separate bump appears that pricks the tendon and is not to your hope in its restoration, then you file it away and stop it according to the way which we have said above.
But if the fracture is with a splinter, then I have already written about that particular chapter, and therefore take its cure.

Chapter 23.
Of speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints of its place, by which it is prohibited from moving, and the figure of the limb is altered, and brings great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when dislocation occurs, let it be immediately forced back into place, and not delayed in being reduced, for if it is delayed, the joint will be inflamed and very strong, and the reduction of the dislocation will be difficult.
And for that reason, one should not be bled or stretched during the hour of inflammation, because many times it brings on spasms and pain to the patient.
But when this happens, let you resort to phlebotomy on the patient, and let him be laid flat so that he may rest the inflammation; and let the limb be bathed with warm water and oil, and let it be reduced with ease.
And every limb shall be cured by what its memory will return to its place.
And now we have ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what was preceded in fractures, from the highest part of the body down to the lowest.
Chapter 24.
Of the cure for dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two mandibles are dislocated if not bitten; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: they are either dislocated from their places by a small movement and slightly softened, or they are fully dislocated and completed, so that they are softened at the joint where saliva runs down the patient's throat, and he cannot hold it in his mouth or close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped around his words.
But when the dislocation of him is slight, then in the greatest dispositions it returns by itself for a slight cause.
But if the dislocation is entire and completed, then let the reduction of it be administered gently, and not delayed at all.
And let the minister hold the patient' head, and let the doctor put one of his fingers in the root of the dislocation of the mandible inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he put both fingers in together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the others outside with which he grips; and command the patient to cease moving his mandibles and relax those everywhere; and let the doctor grip the mandible and press it in until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction of it is difficult, and especially if both mandibles are fully dislocated, then administer evaporation with warm water and oil, so that the reduction of both is made gentle, and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And therefore when they are returned and are closed, and the mouth of the patient is not softened, then pause upon them with a powder of linen with cerot, and let it be made of wax and rozat oil; and let it be bound with ease by mild bonds.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and let his head stand between two pillows, so that he does not bite to the right or left; and do not force him to chew for any reason; having his food be lion's paw, so that when the pains are increased and the mandibles are swollen, then let him bite what he will.
And that shall be administered with ease, and do not work upon the opening of the mouth after eating and drinking and waking, so that the mandible may be swollen and healed.
And if the reduction of two mandibles is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and not returned to their places, then many times it happens that fevers and continuous fever occur, and very strongly the belly of the patient is swollen, and very strongly he vomits pure cholera.
And when you see that, then know that it is a loss; and many times the one to whom that happens dies within ten days.

Chapter 26.

Chapter .XXVII.
Of the cure of dislocation of the neck.
Know that the joint of the neck is dislocated with difficulty and returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated to all parts, and most to the front from the back, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is evident, when you adjust the neck dislocated by the throat, then it is declared to you manifestly, because the joint is concavely and cannot bend the arm or touch with that his own muscle.
And know that your neck joints are dislocated as soon as it comes to you with an abscess cauterized, because if it comes to you with an abscess cauterized, the reduction is difficult, and most of all when it is at the back parts, because above all the other species of dislocation, it has greater pain, and many times it ends in death.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce, is that his hand is stretched with two hands, and his arm is expanded, and the doctor's hands are on the neck and underneath him; and he pushes the joint with his fingertips or the edge of his palm inward so that it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is at the front parts, then it returns as he bends his hand with his own hand, as he thrusts the neck with the edge of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer violent extension and a strong blow.
And two ministers should expand his arm, and hold that joint so that it is not caught due to the extension.
And then his arm is rolled over all parts, because it is wrapped in a long folded cloth over his hand, or bandage.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, he should smear it with some oil so that it aids in lubricating the joint with ease; and then he pushes the joint with violent thrust inward as it returns.
And then its reduction should be that it is placed over that ointment in which it was bound and tightened with liniment, and let it dry with a gentle heat; and his arm is suspended from the neck and left for several days.
And he should be unbound; and if the joint is formed in its place, then you should loosen his ligament and leave it there.
And if you see the joint is not strained, then you should repeat the plaster and the ligament, and leave it for a few days until it is relaxed; and then loosen it.
But if it happens to him gravity in the reduction of the joint, and his disposition is in movement, then administer moisture in bath and subtle friction, and compression, while he is moved away; or pause over the joint ointment, if it is a joint of moving fat; and then it should be bound, and leave it on the bone for day and night. And then stop that, and put him in a bath; and when he is warm, then you should rub the joint with tempered friction, and then repeat over it ointment, if it is a joint of moving, once, and the second, and the third,. Corruption has already occurred in the brain through the instrument of bathing, so it should be treated with liniment.
And if you want, put on that wound of cow's milk and calve it with a string over that; and do this several times, because that is moist and returns to its disposition.
Chapter .XXVIII.
Of the cure of dislocation of the elbow, if it is of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
The elbow of the hand is sometimes displaced, and its reduction from dislocation is easier, and it is the contrary in other joints, but nevertheless there must be velocity in its reduction in the hour in which it is displaced, lest the place should become apoplectic or apoplexy occur in it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you put the joint of the hand of the patient on a table and have the minister extend his hand, and the doctor puts his hand or the palm of his hand on the prominence of the joint, and push it in until it returns to its place.
But nevertheless there must be considered if the palm is already turned to the belly of the hand, then the patient should put his fist on a table opposite to the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is previous to the fist, then the position of the hand of the patient should be intrinically upon the table, so that the hand of the doctor lies over that prominence of the joint.
And therefore if he returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then tighten that with plaster, yielding the apoplexy and leave it; and do not repeat this, because if you do not suffer or cannot bear the reduction of it until several days have passed.
But nevertheless the joint is bent according to its tortuosity; and do not cause any pain to the patient, unless his hand is softened and cannot tighten anything; then you should know that the nerves are torn or cut, and there is no skill in it except that it is tightened with cautery near its joint and tightly bound to it.
Therefore when the elbow is returned, then put over it plaster which we have recommended, and let it be left and bound for several days; and then loosen it and let it be left and the hand of him exercised.
But if its movement is impeded and any heaviness occurs in it, then loosen that with warm water and friction several times, until it is relaxed.
Chapter .XXIX.
Of the cure of dislocation of the fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are completely dislocated.
And when any finger is dislocated on the back of the palm or belly, then press the finger and push its dislocation with your fingers, until it returns.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend that depending on the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend that and extend that until it is straight in that its day.
And when it is night, loosen that according to that narration, and do not cease to loosen it during the day and exercise with movement, and do this in the night; and do this for several days until it is tight.
And do this if it is dislocated to the belly of the hand; and loosen that to that part and do with that your first operation, until it is healed.
And likewise do with that when it is dislocated to other parts.
Chapter .XXX.
Of the cure of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When it happens to any of the vertebrae of the back or neck complete dislocation, and only a few vertebrae remain in their places, then there is no cure for that, because death approaches the patient.
And the sign of that is that the excretion of the patient is outside his will, and he cannot retain it; and often some members of him are softened or both legs or two arms, or one of those; or a vertebra is torn from its place, because often it is torn and often the removal of those to four parts.
Then that is torn to the parts behind and is called hernia.
But the cure of it is that then you consider if the hernia occurs from infancy, because then there is no cure for it nor of that is there no healing at all.
But in that which comes from falling or by beating or by similar to those, then the Ancients have described their ways of curing with long speech, of which the most returns with profit; but I and I have abbreviated that of which the excess of it, by its nearness to my intention has exposed me to that, on which I have expounded and formed various instruments.
And of those none is cured except that which occurs on the back precisely with that which I have described.
And let the patient be extended upon his face upon a flat pillow, and let him be extended below a wet cloth so that his feet are not cold.
And then a straight staff should be placed, fixt in the earth at the end of the pillow around his head; and another staff in the air beneath his feet at the other end of the pillow.
And one minister should hold the staff, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another minister should hold the other according to that form.
And then wrap around the head of the patient and under the armpits let the lion's tongue be stretched; and extend the end of the tongue to the staff which is opposite his head, and loosen that in him; and then tighten the other ligament over his loins with his fingers, and let it be bound over his feet; and then adjust all the ligaments and loosen those in the other staff which is opposite his feet.
And then the ministers each extend the ligament to the end of the staff, and let not the two staffs be torn and the ligaments of their places in which they are fixt, but nevertheless do not fix them as I have said.
And the doctor put his palms over the vertebra with strength until it returns, or that he lie on a table, and then let him adjust his feet upon the table until he returns.
But if he does not return by this form, then take a table of which the width or length is in the air for three arms, and hollow in the wall of which we have said that it is opposite to the patient a place into which enters the end of the table. Then place the smaller part of the board over the spine, and the doctor places his feet on the other end, and stretch well so that the spine is straightened and returns to its place.
And if you want, open the anus with your hand; and you should put the patient's head on the ground in front of his feet, the length of which is one arm, and his head in front of his heels, two feet; and the length between each post is a palm, and now there is made on each post a hole through which the rope runs, and each is very firmly fixed to the ground so that it is not moved.
And there should be placed a round post in which is the pulley, and it is wound around the two pegs of the two posts.
And on its end is a hole in which the post is formed, the length of which is a palm where it is wound; and on the other two posts there is similar to that.
Then stretch the ligaments that you have tightened on the patient's feet in the hole that is under his head, and the ligaments that you have tightened on his thighs in the pulley that is under his feet.
And then fix each pulley and have an assistant twist the pulley with his hand, and the doctor straightens the spine as we have said.
And when the spine is returned and the place is healed, then cover it with a paste of oil and wax, and stop it up; and then place over the paste a board whose width is three fingers or so, and the length of it is the quantity that covers the area of the hunchbackness, and over the top of the spine.
And then let it be bound with a ligament as we have said, and feed the patient subtle food while he is healing.
And if any swelling remains in the area at the end of the healing, then cover that with medicines that soften and smoothen by application of the board for a long time, which we have recounted; and now it is applied to that place lead sheet.
And sometimes there is swelling in the rear of the spine's two bones, and it is healed by that dislocation; and it is the bone which already swells so that it will not cover him with this medicine, for it will soon cause death to him.
Chapter .xxxi.
Of cure for a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle do not bend but only dislocate, and do not happen to those which happen to other joints of little removal and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated according to four ways, for it is dislocated at the inner parts, or at the outer parts, or at the front parts, or at the back parts.
And mostly it is dislocated at the inner parts, and few at the front or back parts.
And the sign of its dislocation at the inner parts is that when you straighten the patient's leg, it is longer and the joint is more prominent than of the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his foot because of the lengthening, and there is a place which lengthens the joint by an aposteme cauterized, so that the head of the thighbone has come here.
And the sign of that which it happens to dislocate at the outer parts is that its accidents are contrary to those above.
And the sign of that which it happens to dislocate at the front parts is that it extends his leg according to its complement, but indeed does not bend that joint without pain which is in the joint, and if it forces itself to walk and cannot go forward over that towards the front parts; and his urine is retained and his joint is apostematized, and he should walk on his heel.
And the sign of that which it happens to dislocate at the back parts is that it does not extend the joint, nor can bend that joint towards the front which bends the joint, and his leg is shorter than the other one, and his joint is softened, and the head of the thighbone is at that place.
But the reduction of the species of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and antique, which has already been healed by you administering its reduction and remaining according to its disposition, then there should not be done a prosthesis on it.
But in that one of which the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four species of dislocation, then you go to that one you want to turn the joint and extend it at the inner parts and the outer parts, and then to the right and left, for it will soon return and not need another cure.
But if it does not return in this way, then you should prepare a strong minister and extend the leg of him from the bottommost part, or with his fingers, or with a ligament which is tied over the leg below the joint; and another minister extends his hand over that below in such a way as to put his fingers below the nipples of him; and then tighten with a ligament lion over the root of the thighbone, and let another minister press the end of the ligament.
And it should be extended either from the inner part to the antingling of the collarbone, or from the back part to the part of the shoulder blade.
And it should be extended in one go while the patient is raised up by his body from the earth and hangs bent.
And this species of extension is common to all four.
And so if the dislocation returns by what we have said, then all is well; and otherwise, it is necessary for every species that I say about its own cure.
In its own reduction, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is at the inner parts, let the patient be laid on his back; and then place the ligaments over the root of the thighbone in that place between the head of the thighbone and the part which is below the joint; and then extend the ligament above from the part of the antingling to the highest part of the collarbone.
And then another strong minister should take the armpits of him, and hold up the large place of the patient's thighbone and extend to the outer parts by violent extension, for that returns in its place.
And this species is of further lengthier cure by which this member is reduced than the other species.
But if it is excused from you and does not obey the treatment by this species of cure of all, then let his two legs be bound together with a strong ligament, lengthier, over both heels and over both joints.
And let the length of each of his parts be the quantity of four fingers, and let the leg of him be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
And then let the patient be suspended over the head of the post, and let it be in the middle; and let its length from the earth be the quantity of two arms.
And then command a strong youth to shorten the head of the thighbone, and let it be suspended by another youth, and that other youth shorten the thighbone with strength, for the joint will return to its place with speed.
But in the true reduction when it is a dislocation to the outside, let the patient lie on his side as we have recommended with the elbow bent; and let the ligament be stretched properly over the patient's chest and over their feet.
And let two poles be placed, one at the patient's feet and the other at their head; and let poles be placed under their buttocks, with a cloth moistened in vinegar wrapped around the pole so that the patient is not harmed by the pole being between their thighs, so that the extension to the lower parts is not drawn.
And let each minister extend to their side, and the doctor have his hand on the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is to the front parts, then let the chest be stretched slightly, and it is according to this narrative on the elbow; and let the doctor place the palm of his right hand over the painful shoulder, and let him tightly hold that with his other hand, and with this tight grip he extends that to the lower parts to the tip of the toe.
And if the dislocation is to the back parts, then let the patient not be extended to the lower parts, and they are elevated above the earth; unless it is placed on a hard surface, as well as how we have said about the placement of it on two arms; and they are on their face and their ligaments are stretched, according to what we have said, there is no harm.
And let compression be administered with a table over the place to which you turn the joint.
And when the reduction is done according to what you see — and the sign of the reduction is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend the patient's two arms, and when you see those bent and the patient stretches his arm and expands without any excuse, then you know that the joint according to what it is returned — and then you adjust both thighs and apply plaster and stretch with a bandage so that the joint is not damaged in any of the parts; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
And then loosen the ligament and the plaster, and compare one arm to the other.
Then, if you see those bent in truth, then you know that the dislocation is already closed, and then let the patient walk.
And if you see any cause of softening, then return and plaster that, and stretch it according to your first stretching, and let that be for three days.
And then loosen that, and have that delay in walking on any day, until it is comforted.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder joint.
The shoulder joint is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated to the outside parts, and to the inside parts, and to the lower parts but not to the front parts.
And the sign of dislocation of it is that you command the patient to apply their arm to their thigh; then you know that the shoulder joint is dislocated.
And the restoration of all its ways is that you make the patient lie straight, so that they already extend their arm, if they can on that; adhere to the patient's two cheeks with your hands, and place the patient's foot between your feet; then let another minister extend the patient's arm while you hold their shoulder with your palms, and another minister extends the patient's leg while the doctor applies reduction to the shoulder joint with strength.
And the sign of its reduction is that their arm adheres to their thigh without laborious lifting.
And then plaster that and have the arm adhere to the thigh, and then bind those together with a bandage for three or four days, and then loosen them.
And do not let the patient walk for several days, until it is healed and comforted.
But if its reduction to you is excused, then administer strong extension with the ligaments from which in the treatment of the hip joint there is preceding narrative.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the ankle joint.
The ankle joint is sometimes dislocated by small removal, and sometimes it is dislocated according to completion, and its dislocation is made either to the inside parts or to the outside parts.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the ankle swollen proceeding from the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its removal is of slight reduction, and it is extended with facility by hands and bent while it returns.
But in its treatment when it is dislocated according to completion, let the patient lie straight, and have a strong and valiant minister from behind their back in the middle location of them.
And then you with your right hand take the ankle from the highest part, and with your left hand from the lowest part of the ankle in the location of the ankle bone.
And then pull the ankle to you with your right hand, and then with the left twice, and then extend with the left; and press the foot of the ankle to you with the right behind the arm in its labor.
And do that two times according to what we have counted.
And then press the foot of the ankle to the calf the third time, and you are at the ankle bone.
Therefore if it returns in one time or two according to this way, and if you see the ankle bent, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it, for it will return.
But if to you the reduction of it is prohibited according to what we have recommended, then let the patient lie on their back on earth, and stretch a wooden stake in firm earth, and that it be placed between the patient's thighs, and you already wrapped a cloth around it so that the patient does not slip.
And then the minister of it has his thighs, and then another minister extends the arm with their hands or with the ligament that lies on the neck of the ankle; and then each minister extends against the extension of their fellow, and the stake is extended between the patient's thighs holding them so that the body of him is not drawn to the lower parts because of his extension.
And then the doctor applies reduction with their hands, and another minister has the healthy thigh to the lower parts, for the dislocation returns with facility.
And then when the dislocation returns and you are informed of its reduction, then apply plaster and stretch it firmly with ligaments, and tie the ankle to the calf.
And let you beware or keep watch on the nerves which are behind on the ankle, so that the ligament over it is not vehement and that one does not lead it strongly.
And then let him lie for two or three days; and if the ligament is softened, stretch it.
And then loosen that on the third day or on the fourth, and prohibit the patient from walking for 40 days. But if it is forced to go in front of that space, then there is no guarantee that the dislocation is not dissolved and corrupted in it, and so it receives no cure.
And if it happens that the wound turns bad, then you must apply in the treatment of it that which we described earlier, in another place of cure and of bandaging, until it is healed.
Chapter 34.
Of the cure of dislocation of the big toe of the foot.
Let him who is dislocated be anointed with a little oil, and that which is not difficult, rather let it be lighter.
But if the dislocation is in one of the joints of the big toe of the foot, then let him have his foot cut off from under him and lie on the ground or a table, and remain there as if he were walking.
Then you stand on your feet and place your foot over what is causing the pain from the joints, and then press that with your foot with strength until it returns, and see if it has been anointed and there is no lump.
Then place under his foot a board that supports the whole foot, which has two ends, and then stretch it with decent and firm tension for three days; and then loosen it and let him rest from walking for several days, until he is restored and his healing is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the kinds of dislocation that are done by plague or by fracture or by any other cause.
When one of those causes occurs, and you persevere in the cure and restoration of him, many times death ensues.
And so you must not presume upon the cure of him, but let him who is wise in the art, with great skill, compassion, and pity, be slow, not hasty or presumptuous, and use at the beginning of medicines that soothe bad wounds only slightly, and let the patient depend on divine judgment, unless you hope for salvation from loss, from the alleviation of illness, and see some hope in it. And then immediately strive to reduce him at the beginning of the cause, before he develops a bad wound.
And then if the member returns as we want, then administer treatment that soothes bad wounds and cures the plague with what is suitable for him of the dry ointments.
And if the dislocation is by fracture and the bones are separated, then you must persevere in the treatment of them, and do in those what we have said earlier about the simple illnesses of those.
And guard and avoid, according to your ability, that from the instrument in the ways of fear as it is foretold to you in my prophecy; for that is better for you, and more beneficial to your true glory, and more healthful for your real body, those in your time. Thus ends the words of Al-Bukasi perfectly, of which God be praised. Amen.
